{"nick":"joepie91","message":"but you should really only do that for actually-schemaless data; you can't *make* data schemaless, and trying to store schemaful data in a schemaless storage format just means that your application ends up defining an implicit schema, which is harder to maintain and easier to fuck up","date":"2018-03-19T13:39:25.280Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"tmiland","date":"2018-03-19T13:39:26.009Z","type":"join"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"Peasant65: two reasons","date":"2018-03-19T13:39:31.888Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"1) you want the integrity guarantees as close to your 'point of storage' as possible, to ensure that misbehaving clients / mismatched versions / etc. can't accidentally wreck your data; having the guarantees directly in the database is therefore optimal, as it makes it literally *impossible* for anything to store invalid data","date":"2018-03-19T13:40:11.562Z","type":"message"}
{"nick":"joepie91","message":"2) the database can do these kind of checks much more performantly than your application can; not just because there's usually over a decade of engineering and optimizations behind it, but also because no additional network requests are necessary to do so","date":"2018-03-19T13:40:44.636Z","type":"message"}
